        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.8b01888"><meta property="og:title" content="Three-Dimensional Hierarchical Framework Assembled by Cobblestone-Like CoSe2@C Nanospheres for Ultrastable Sodium-Ion Storage"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/acsami.8b01888.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), as the promising commercial energy system, are restricted by their sluggish kinetics and low sodium-ion storage. Metal selenide possesses good conductivity and capacity but still suffers from the stacked problem and volume expansion. Significantly, CoSe2/C is successfully prepared with the assistance of citric acid as both a chelating agent and carbon precursor, displaying that cobblestone-like nanospheres with the radii (<25 nm) distribute uniformly in the carbon matrix. It is expected that the established Co–O–C bonds enhance the stability of the structure with faster ion shuttling. With the available electrolyte (NaCF3SO3/diethylene glycol dimethyl ether) in a potential window range from 0.5 to 3.0 V, the as-obtained sample shows the ultralong lifespan at 4.5 A g–1, retaining a capacity of 345 mA h g–1 after 10 000 cycles. From the detailed kinetic analysis, it is clear that the surface-controlled electrochemical behavior mainly contributes to the excellent large-current cycling stability and Na storage capacity. The ex situ results support that the crystal and morphological structure remains stable. This work is anticipated to enhance the in-depth understanding of the CoSe2/C anode and supply a facile manner to obtain electrode materials for SIBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/acsami.8b01888.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="9318882b2b3f54b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318882b2b3f54b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '5187aea9-186d-4e47-8859-be09101c4aa2'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318882b2b3f54b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b01888&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acsami.8b01888" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite <script nonce="9318882b2b3f54b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Three-Dimensional Hierarchical Framework Assembled by Cobblestone-Like CoSe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanospheres for Ultrastable Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b01888", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Three-Dimensional Hierarchical Framework Assembled by Cobblestone-Like CoSe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanospheres for Ultrastable Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b01888", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Three-Dimensional Hierarchical Framework Assembled by Cobblestone-Like CoSe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanospheres for Ultrastable Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b01888", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Three-Dimensional Hierarchical Framework Assembled by Cobblestone-Like CoSe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanospheres for Ultrastable Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b01888", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Three-Dimensional Hierarchical Framework Assembled by Cobblestone-Like CoSe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanospheres for Ultrastable Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b01888", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Three-Dimensional Hierarchical Framework Assembled by Cobblestone-Like CoSe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanospheres for Ultrastable Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b01888", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Three-Dimensional Hierarchical Framework Assembled by Cobblestone-Like CoSe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanospheres for Ultrastable Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b01888", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Three-Dimensional Hierarchical Framework Assembled by Cobblestone-Like CoSe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanospheres for Ultrastable Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b01888", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Three-Dimensional Hierarchical Framework Assembled by Cobblestone-Like CoSe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanospheres for Ultrastable Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b01888", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

        </div></div><div class="article__pubdate"><span>Research Article</span><time datetime="2018-04-11">April 11, 2018</time></div><h1 class="article_header-title" tabindex="0"><span class="hlFld-Title" property="headline">Three-Dimensional Hierarchical Framework Assembled by Cobblestone-Like CoSe<sub>2</sub>@C Nanospheres for Ultrastable Sodium-Ion Storage</span><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy article link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Article link copied!</span></div></h1><ul class="loa"><li><span><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor" tabindex="0" data-id="article_author_info" property="author">Peng Ge</span><div class="loa-info hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Peng Ge</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">State Key Laboratory for Power Metallurgy &nbsp;and&nbsp; Metal selenide possesses good conductivity and capacity but still suffers from the stacked problem and volume expansion. Significantly, CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C is successfully prepared with the assistance of citric acid as both a chelating agent and carbon precursor, displaying that cobblestone-like nanospheres with the radii (&lt;25 nm) distribute uniformly in the carbon matrix. It is expected that the established Co–O–C bonds enhance the stability of the structure with faster ion shuttling. With the available electrolyte (NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>/diethylene glycol dimethyl ether) in a potential window range from 0.5 to 3.0 V, the as-obtained sample shows the ultralong lifespan at 4.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, retaining a capacity of 345 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 10 000 cycles. From the detailed kinetic analysis, it is clear that the surface-controlled electrochemical behavior mainly contributes to the excellent large-current cycling stability and Na storage capacity. The ex situ results support that the crystal and morphological structure remains stable. This work is anticipated to enhance the in-depth understanding of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C anode and supply a facile manner to obtain electrode materials for SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D14716%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DPeng%2BGe%252C%2BHongshuai%2BHou%252C%2BSijie%2BLi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D17%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b01888%26title%3DThree-Dimensional%2BHierarchical%2BFramework%2BAssembled%2Bby%2BCobblestone-Like%2BCoSe2%2540C%2BNanospheres%2Bfor%2BUltrastable%2BSodium-Ion%2BStorage%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D14726%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291337&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon"> Carbon </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties"> Electrical properties </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions"> Ions </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291675&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Stability"> Stability </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cose2%2Fc&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_CoSe2%2FC">CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Co%E2%88%92o%E2%88%92c+Bond&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Co%E2%88%92O%E2%88%92C%20bond">Co−O−C bond</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ultralong+Cycling+Stability&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ultralong%20cycling%20stability">ultralong cycling stability</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Kinetics+Behaviors&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_kinetics%20behaviors">kinetics behaviors</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Restricted by the large radius (0.102 nm) of sodium ions, the available electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with enormous commercial potential have captured numerous attentions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1−5)</a> Learning from lithium-ion batteries, transition-metal-based materials were deemed as the promising materials with high Na storage capacity, derived from the conversion reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 5 to 7">(5−7)</a> Note that the anions (group VI, O, S, and Se) in the compounds serve as important roles in electronic conductivity and electrochemical properties. Among these materials, metal oxide possesses the largest theoretical capacity; however, the generated compact SEI films in the reaction would hinder the Na-ion diffusion entrance, leading to the dissatisfied capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8,9)</a> Owing to the “shuttle effect” of polysulfide with the deteriorated electrochemical properties, the breakthrough has not been achieved on ultralong-term cycles in metal sulfides. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Compared to those aforementioned materials, metal selenide displays a narrow band gap with good conductivity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> Moreover, with the suitable electrolyte, the reaction energy barrier is lowered, facilitating faster electron transfer. Although some capacity would be sacrificed on account of the upgraded sodiation/desodiation platforms, the well cycling stability could be largely enhanced. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 12 to 14">(12−14)</a> For instances, the excellent electrochemical properties (372 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 2000 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>) were exhibited by sphere FeSe<sub>2</sub> with the utilization of the electrolyte NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>/diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DEGDME). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> The nanooctahedra NiSe<sub>2</sub> through hydrothermal reaction displays stable cycling, showing 313 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 4000 cycles at 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> The urchin-like CoSe<sub>2</sub> has been reported by Chen’s group, delivering a Na storage capacity of 410 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 1800 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> However, the volume expansion with pulverization and weak dissolution of polyselenium should not be neglected, perhaps deteriorating the electrode samples, which could bring inferior electrochemical performances. Two controlled strategies were employed to further impart promotion: (1) reducing the particle size can increase the contacting area with the electrolyte, shorten the ion diffusion path, and lower the areal current density and (2) introducing the carbon layer could protect the direct erosion of the electrolyte, alleviate volume swelling, and inhibit pulverization. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> The solvothermal reaction or the complicated precursor was frequently used, and it is obvious that the complex manner is not suitable for practical applications. Thus, constructing a three-dimensional (3D) structure composed of zero-dimensional (0D) metal selenium/carbon is of importance through the facile fabricated process, which significantly optimizes the properties of target samples and realizes their commercial values. Moreover, introduction of M(metal)–O/N–C bonds acts a significant role in imparting the improvement on the structure stability and conductivity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 19 to 21">(19−21)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">As a familiar complexing agent, citric acid has been widely used to obtain uniform particles with the carbon layer. Recent report demonstrated that citric acid was pyrolyzed to form dual carbon layer coating Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Ag/TiO<sub>2</sub> was also dispersed in carbon because of the effective complexing agent. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> The plentiful −COOH groups would increase the ability of forming uniform complex and M–O–C bonds in the final product.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this paper, benefitting from the dual effect of critic acid as both a chelating agent and carbon precursor, the 3D framework assembled by 0D CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C with the small radii (&lt;25 nm) was successfully obtained, accompanying with the introduction of the Co–O–C bonds. The obtained sample delivers ultralong-term stability, maintaining the capacity of 345 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 10 000 cycles at 4.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> with the coulombic efficiency (CE) (98%). The excellent electrochemical properties can be attributed to the effective stable structure, ether-based electrolytes, and capacitive-controlled behaviors.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Chemicals</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All of the chemicals were purchased from Aladdin Chemical and used without any purification.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Synthesis of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C Structure</h3><div class="NLM_p last">In brief, 0.5 g of critic acid, 1.0 g of Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O (&gt;99%), and 1.0 g of Se (&gt;99%) were mixed in an agate mortar. Then, the materials were ground adequately for 30 min for homogeneous mixing. The uniform mixture was placed in the porcelain boat and then transferred to the tube furnace. The starting conditions were at 500 °C for 2 h with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> at H<sub>2</sub>/Ar (5%:95%). After natural cooling, the product was milled for 30 min to obtain the final samples CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Characterization of the Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The crystal structures were probed by X-ray diffraction (XRD), Cu Kα radiation at λ = 1.54 Å, Rigaku powder diffractometer (DMAXB) X-ray spectrometer, standard samples (LaB6 powder), and Raman spectra (Renishaw INVIA micro-Raman spectroscopy). Using the thermogravimetric analysis, the content was determined through 30–800 °C with 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> at atmosphere. The specific surface area and pore distribution were measured by Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (Micromeritics, ASAP 2020). The morphology and detailed internal structure were characterized by field emission scanning electron microscopy (SEM, JSM-7600) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM, JEM-2010F). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was performed to analyze the valence state.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Electrochemical Properties</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The obtained samples, carboxymethylcellulose sodium, and super carbon with the mass ratio of 70:15:15 were mixed uniformly in deionized water. Then, the slurry was coated onto the Cu foil and further dried at 80 °C for 12 h. The foil was tailored to the plate with a diameter of 1 cm, and the active mass can reach to 1.0–1.5 mg. The counter electrode was the metallic sodium foil, and the electrolyte was 1 M NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> in DEGDME (diethylene glycol dimethyl ether). The CR2016 coin cells were assembled in the argon-filled glovebox. The galvanostatic discharge–charge was conducted by land battery systems (5 V, 10 mA) in the potential from 0.5 to 3.0 V at different current densities. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) were performed by MULTI AUTOLAB M204.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, the XRD patterns of the as-obtained CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C and C were displayed, which are indexed to the standard PDF#88-1712, revealing that the sample belongs to the pure cubic trogtalite CoSe<sub>2</sub>, <i>Pa</i>3̅(205) space group, <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = <i>c</i> = 5.8593 Å. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> In addition, the peaks located at 30.48°, 34.19°, 37.57°, 43.65°, 51.70°, 56.59°, and 58.93° were noticed. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/suppl_file/am8b01888_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, they are corresponding to (200), (210), (211), (220), (311), (023), and (321) lattice planes, respectively, indicating the well-crystalline state. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> According to the Scatter’s equation (<i>D</i><sub><i>hkl</i></sub> = <i>K</i>λ/β cos θ; <i>K</i> = 0.9 and λ = 1.5406 Å), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 26 to 28">(26−28)</a> the rough size of crystalline grains was about 10 nm. Moreover, the peaks of carbon were not found in the XRD pattern of CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C, which is ascribed to the high peak intensity of CoSe<sub>2</sub>. Raman spectra were performed to further investigate the detailed crystal structure and the existed carbon. The D peak appeared at 1364 cm<sup>–1</sup> and the G peak located at 1596 cm<sup>–1</sup> are associated with sp<sup>3</sup> and sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized carbon, and <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> is about 0.85, revealing the high graphitic degree. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 29 to 31">(29−31)</a> The peak at 673 cm<sup>–1</sup>, marked as A<sub>1g</sub>, is the characteristic peak of CoSe<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> To confirm the content of carbon, the tested TG/DSC curves and the XRD pattern of the residual product are exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c,d, respectively. At the temperature of 403 °C, CoSe<sub>2</sub> was transformed into CoSe with the sublimation of SeO<sub>2</sub>. In addition, the carbon was converted to CO<sub>2</sub> at 429 °C. When the temperature increased to 591 °C, CoSe was decomposed into Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, leaving SeO<sub>2</sub>. On the basis of the equation (the content of carbon = 1 – 3<i>W</i><sub>Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub></sub> × <i>M</i><sub>CoSe<sub>2</sub></sub>/<i>M</i><sub>Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub></sub>), the content of CoSe<sub>2</sub> is about 82.6% and the content of carbon was 17.4%. The internal valence bonds of CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C were preliminary evaluated by Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectra in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f and S1. Compared to that of cobalt acetylacetonate (blue lines) containing the Co–O–C bond at ∼600 cm<sup>–1</sup>, it is found that the same peak of the target materials appeared, indicating the formation of Co–O–C bond, allowing the stable structure and facile electron transfer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20 ref34" aria-label="reference 19, 20 and 34">(19,20,34)</a> In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e, the process of the electron transferring from carbon to the CoSe<sub>2</sub> crystal lattice was displayed. Co–O–C as the bridge could facilitate faster ion shuttling. Thus, this bond was further investigated as follows.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/medium/am-2018-01888n_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/large/am-2018-01888n_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Chemical structure properties of CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C: (a) XRD patterns, (b) Raman spectra, (c) TG/DSC curves, (d) XRD patterns of residual products, (e) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption isotherms, (f) FTIR spectra, and (g) crystalline structure and ion shuttling.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/large/am-2018-01888n_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b01888&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Further analyzing the valence bonds of CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C through XPS, the detailed chemical interaction between CoSe<sub>2</sub> and carbon matrix is found in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>. Clearly, the peaks of Co 2p, C 1s, Se 3d, and O 1s were displayed in the full spectrum of the sample, confirming the existence of the above-mentioned elements. The high resolutions of C, Se, and O elements were probed to evaluate the chemical states. It is found that the peak of Co 2p is composed of Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> approximately located at 792 and 777 eV, which contain the Co–Se and Se–Co–Se bonds, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 25, 32 and 33">(25,32,33)</a> In addition, the Se 3d peak can be deconvoluted into five peaks: Se 3d<sub>5/2</sub>, Se 3d<sub>3/2</sub>, Co 3p<sub>3/2</sub>, Co 3p<sub>1/2</sub>, and SeO<sub>2</sub>, which mainly appeared at 54.3, 55.3, 57.4, 58.8, and 60.4 eV, respectively. Among them, the Co–Se bonds are indexed to the peak at 54.3 eV. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> The existing slight SeO<sub>2</sub> is ascribed to that some Se on the surface was oxidized to some extent. Importantly, according to the previous work, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 20, 35 and 36">(20,35,36)</a> the peak at 285 eV is associated with Co–O–C, and those at 283, 286, and 286 eV are in relation with C–C, C–O, and C═O bonds, respectively, in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d. Meanwhile, the Co–O–C bond at 532.8 eV is also found in the spectrum of O 1s, and other peaks are C–O at 535 eV, C═O at 531 eV, and the adsorbed O<sub>2</sub> from atmosphere at 530 eV. This XPS analysis can effectively verify the existence of Co–O–C bonds.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/medium/am-2018-01888n_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/large/am-2018-01888n_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. XPS spectrum of CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C: (a) full spectrum, (b) Co 2p, (c) Se 3d, (d) C 1s, and (e) O 1s.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/large/am-2018-01888n_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b01888&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The 0D to 3D hierarchical cobalt selenide framework is confirmed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a,b. Clearly, some pores were observed in the sample, facilitating the infiltration of the electrolyte, thereby shortening the ion diffusion paths. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a> As shown by the SEM images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c, it is found that the unique framework is composed of 0D cobblestone-like nanospheres, which effectively increase the active sites of the target sample. The nanospheres were dispersed homogeneously in the carbon matrix in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d. In addition, the radius of nanospheres (&lt;25 nm) is clear in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e, which is beneficial for that of the CoSe<sub>2</sub> reacted with Na<sup>+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref39" aria-label="reference 33 and 39">(33,39)</a> In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f, the clear graphitic lattice space is about 0.34 nm and the lattice fringe of CoSe<sub>2</sub> is 0.26 nm, corresponding to the (210) facet (JCPDs: 88-1712). In comparison of the SAED images about carbon, the diffraction rings of CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C are indexed to (200), (211), and (311) for CoSe<sub>2</sub>; meanwhile, that of graphitic planes (002) is exhibited. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> The elements of Co, Se, C, and O were distributed uniformly in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>i. Clearly, the atomic ratio of Co/Se is 1:2, further verifying that the target product CoSe<sub>2</sub> is obtained.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/medium/am-2018-01888n_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/large/am-2018-01888n_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Morphological structure of CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C: SEM images at different magnifications (a–c), TEM images (d,e), HRTEM image (f) and SAED image (g) of CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C and carbon, mapping images (h), and EDX spectrum (i).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/large/am-2018-01888n_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b01888&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">From the aforementioned discussion, the corresponding formation is proposed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. With the increased temperature, citric acid was first molten. Benefitting from its complexing function, the Co<sup>2+</sup> ions were contacted with the citric acid to form −COO–Co. Subsequently, 5% H<sub>2</sub> renders some Co<sup>2+</sup> reduced to Co particles which were distributed uniformly in the complexing agent. Accompanying with the improvement of temperature, the citric acid was carbonized to form a 3D framework structure and the pores were created by leaving some oxygen. Meanwhile, Se with H<sub>2</sub> was changed to H<sub>2</sub>Se, further reacting with Co to obtain CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/medium/am-2018-01888n_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/large/am-2018-01888n_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Formation mechanism of CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/large/am-2018-01888n_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b01888&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The detailed Na storage behaviors of CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C were performed as an anode for SIBs in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>. The tested potential range was from 0.5 to 3.0 V, which is ascribed to that (1) few capacity was existed in the voltage between 0 and 0.5 V and (2) some side reactions would be evoked at low potential, resulting in the poor cycling stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> On the basis of the previous report, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> the capacity of bare CoSe<sub>2</sub> is only about 300 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Surprisingly, as exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, the as-prepared sample shows the initial discharge/charge capacities of 545 and 467 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, yielding a CE of 85.6%. These advantages may be due to the unique nanostructure that brings about the adequate active sites. The capacity of 46 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> was found for the pure carbon from citric acid. Note that the high initial CE is in favor of the practical application. From the 2nd to 75th cycles, owing to the active process and side reactions of the electrode, the decreased part was displayed. Subsequently, the stable cycling is obvious, and no fading can be found from 100th to 500th cycles, delivering a capacity of 428 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with a CE of 100.3%, resulted from the designed significant structure and the introduction of carbon with Co–O–C bonds. It is clear that a couple of oxidation/reduction platforms were located at 2.1 and 1.1 V, agreeing well with the CV curves in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/suppl_file/am8b01888_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>. The peak at 1.3 eV was found at the first CV curves, which is attributed to the formation of the SEI film. Moreover, three discharge platforms were found at 1.6, 1.1, and 0.7 V and two charge platforms were suited at 1.5 and 1.8 V for 100th, 200th, and 500th cycles, demonstrating the stable reaction of the electrode as listed by the corresponding reaction process (S1, S2, S3, S4, and S5) in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>, which have been demonstrated by the previous report. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> The rate capability was evaluated at various current densities of 0.2, 0.4, 0.8, 1.6, 3.2, and 6.4 A g<sup>–1</sup>, maintaining capacities of 484, 447, 380, 335, 304, and 296 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. When the current density comes back to 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity could recover to 355 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, implying its good reversibility. In addition, the excellent rate property was confirmed by the stable platforms at stepwise current densities in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d, revealing the lower pulverization. Impressively, after 10 000 cycles at 4.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the Na storage capacity still remains 345 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with ∼100% retention based on the 1000th cycle, suggesting the ultralong-term cycling stability and enormous practical promising, which is better than the previous reports in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a>. In addition, the bare CoSe<sub>2</sub> with graphene displayed the relative low capacity of 280 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 4.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/suppl_file/am8b01888_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f, the stable platforms from 6000th to 6010th loops and two sodiation/desodiation processes were completed in 1127 s at 4.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The advantages of the electrode samples were the effective framework structure, uniform carbon matrix, and stable Co–O–C bonds.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/medium/am-2018-01888n_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/large/am-2018-01888n_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical properties of CoSe<sub>2</sub>/C and C: (a) cycling stability and (b) sodiation/desodiation curves at the current density of 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Rate capability at various current densities and (d) discharge/charge platforms. (e) Ultralong-term cycling stability at a large current density of 4.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> and (f) voltage–time curves from 6000 to 6010 loops.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b01888/asset/images/large/am-2018-01888n_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b01888&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1.
